So f(n) divides (f(1)^{f(n)} - 1). If f(1) = 1, then it's 0, so trivial. If f(1) > 1, then we can argue that f(n) ≤ something like (f(1)^{f(n)} - 1) but that's not an upper bound.

Alternatively, maybe we can prove that f(n) ≤ n for all n by considering a = 2 and b such that f(b) is large. But maybe we can find a contradiction if f(n) > n. Let's try to systematically analyze.

We have:

(1) For all a,b: f(a) | (b^a - f(b)^{f(a)}).

Since f(a) divides the difference, it also divides any linear combination. In particular, for fixed a, we can consider b modulo f(a). For any b, b^a ≡ f(b)^{f(a)} (mod f(a)). That means that the function g(b) = f(b)^{f(a)} (mod f(a)) is determined by b^a. But we can try to pick b such that b is a multiple of f(a). Then b^a ≡ 0 (mod f(a)). So f(b)^{f(a)} ≡ 0 (mod f(a)). Thus f(a) divides f(b)^{f(a)}. Since f(a) > 0, this implies that every prime factor of f(a) divides f(b). So for any b that is a multiple of f(a), f(b) is a multiple of rad(f(a)). That doesn't bound.

Now consider a such that f(a) is large. If we pick b = a, we have f(a) | a^a - f(a)^{f(a)}. Since f(a) is large, maybe we can use the fact that a^a is much smaller than f(a)^{f(a)} if f(a) > a, but that's not a contradiction. The divisibility is a congruence.

Another direction: The condition must hold for all a,b. So we can fix b and vary a. For each b, we have that f(a) divides b^a - f(b)^{f(a)}. This is reminiscent of "f(a) divides something" where the exponent f(a) appears. Perhaps we can consider a = 1,2,... and try to derive that f(n) is bounded by n.

Let's try to find a necessary condition: For any a, choose b = a+1. Then f(a) | (a+1)^a - f(a+1)^{f(a)}. Not obviously bounding.

Maybe we can find a lower bound for f(a) in terms of a. For instance, if f(a) is too large, then consider b = f(a). Then we have f(a) divides f(a)^a - f(f(a))^{f(a)}. That's not helpful.

Better: Consider a = n and b = 1. Then f(n) divides 1 - f(1)^{f(n)}. So f(n) divides (f(1)^{f(n)} - 1). If f(1) = 1, then it's 0, so no restriction. So if f(1) > 1, then we have a strong restriction. Let's denote d = f(1). Then for all n, f(n) divides (d^{f(n)} - 1). In particular, for n such that f(n) > 1, we have that f(n) divides d^{f(n)} - 1. That is like f(n) is a divisor of (d^{f(n)} - 1). This is reminiscent of the concept of "Fermat's little theorem" for base d modulo f(n) if f(n) is prime, but we are not given primality. However we can consider the order of d modulo f(n) divides f(n). Since d^{f(n)} ≡ 1 (mod f(n)). Therefore, the multiplicative order of d modulo f(n) (if gcd(d,f(n)) = 1) divides f(n). But we also need to consider if gcd(d,f(n)) > 1. If f(n) shares a prime factor p with d, then p divides d^{f(n)} - 1. Since p divides d, then d^{f(n)} ≡ 0 mod p, so d^{f(n)} - 1 ≡ -1 mod p, not 0. Therefore, p cannot divide f(n) unless f(n) divides something that is -1 mod p, impossible. So gcd(d,f(n)) must be 1. Indeed, if a prime p divides f(n) and also divides d, then p divides d^{f(n)} (since p divides d), so d^{f(n)} ≡ 0 mod p, then d^{f(n)} - 1 ≡ -1 mod p, so p does not divide d^{f(n)} - 1. But f(n) divides (d^{f(n)} - 1). Since p divides f(n), then p must also divide (d^{f(n)} - 1) (because if f(n) divides N, then any prime divisor of f(n) divides N). So we get a contradiction unless p does not divide d^{f(n)} - 1. Therefore, gcd(d,f(n)) = 1. So indeed f(n) is coprime to d.

Thus we have: For all n, f(n) is coprime to f(1). And f(n) divides (f(1)^{f(n)} - 1). So we can apply Euler's theorem: Since gcd(f(1), f(n)) = 1, we have f(1)^{φ(f(n))} ≡ 1 (mod f(n)). But we have exponent f(n). This doesn't directly bound f(n) relative to f(1). However we can consider that f(n) divides (f(1)^{f(n)} - 1). This implies that the order of f(1) modulo f(n) divides f(n). In particular, if f(n) is prime, then by FLT, f(1)^{f(n)-1} ≡ 1 mod f(n). Then f(1)^{f(n)} ≡ f(1) mod f(n). For f(n) to divide f(1)^{f(n)} - 1, we need f(1) ≡ 1 mod f(n). That seems unlikely unless f(n) divides f(1)-1. But we don't know f(n) is prime. But we can try to bound f(n) in terms of f(1) maybe by considering the inequality: f(n) ≤ f(1)^{f(n)} - 1. That's trivial because f(1)^{f(n)} - 1 is huge. But we need an upper bound like f(n) ≤ c n. Claim: For any bonza function f, f(n) ≤ n for all n ∈ N. If this holds, then c = 1 is the smallest constant. But is it always true? For b=1, 1 - f(1)^3. f(1) could be 1 or something. If f(1)=1, then 1-1=0, ok. For b=2, 4 - f(2)^3 = 4 - 27 = -23, not divisible by 3. So fails. So f(2) cannot be 3. But maybe there is some other function where f(2) is larger than 2, but then f(2) must divide 2^2 - f(2)^{f(2)}. That's a self-consistency condition. Let's examine.

Let f(2) = d. Then condition with a=2, b=2 gives: d divides 2^2 - d^d = 4 - d^d. So d | (4 - d^d). That implies d | (4 - d^d). Since d^d is huge, this is a strong condition. For d > 4, d^d is huge, and 4 - d^d is negative, but divisibility holds. For example, d=5: 4 - 5^5 = 4 - 3125 = -3121. Does 5 divide -3121? 3121 mod 5 = 1, so no. So d=5 fails. d=6: 4 - 6^6 = 4 - 46656 = -46652. 6 divides? 46652 mod 6 = 46652 mod 6 = (6*7775=46650) remainder 2, so no. d=7: 4 - 7^7 = 4 - 823543 = -823539. 7 divides? 823539 mod 7 = 7*117647 = 823529, remainder 10, so no. d=8: 4 - 8^8 = 4 - 16777216 = -16777212. 8 divides? 16777212 mod 8 = 4? So far many d fail. But maybe some d > 2 satisfy d | (4 - d^d). That seems unlikely. Let's analyze generally.

We have d divides (4 - d^d). So d divides (4 - d^d). Since d divides d^d, it also divides (4 - d^d) + d^d = 4. So d divides 4. Indeed, if d divides A and also divides (A - B) then it divides B? Thus we have:

∀ a ∈ N: f(a) | a^a. (2)

That's a strong bound. For a = 1, f(1) divides 1^1 = 1, so f(1) = 1. Wait 1^1 = 1, so f(1) must divide 1, thus f(1) = 1. That's a key observation. Thus it appears that the function defined as:

f(n) = 1 for odd n,
f(2) = 4,
f(n) = 2 for even n ≥ 4,

is a bonza function. Let's double-check some edge cases: n=1 (odd) → f(1)=1. n=2 (even) → f(2)=4. n=3 (odd) → f(3)=1. n=4 (even) → f(4)=2. n=5 (odd) → f(5)=1. n=6 (even) → f(6)=2. n=7 (odd) → f(7)=1. n=8 (even) → f(8)=2. etc.

Now, is there any a,b such that f(a) does not divide b^a - f(b)^{f(a)}? We need to check systematically.

We have:

- If a is odd, f(a)=1. Then condition becomes 1 divides b^a - f(b)^1 = b^a - f(b). That's always true. So all pairs with a odd are automatically satisfied.

- If a is even, f(a) is either 4 (a=2) or 2 (a≥4 even). So we need to check for a even:

Case 1: a = 2, f(2) = 4. Then condition: 4 | (b^2 - f(b)^4) for all b ∈ N.

Case 2: a ≥ 4 even, f(a) = 2. Then condition: 2 | (b^a - f(b)^2) for all b ∈ N.

Now, we need to check that these divisibilities hold for all b. Let's analyze.

For a ≥ 4 even, f(a)=2. Then we need 2 divides b^a - f(b)^2. Since b^a is odd if b is odd, even if b is even. f(b) is either 1 (if b odd) or 2 or 4 (if b even). But f(b)^2 mod 2: any integer squared mod 2 is its parity, so if f(b) is even, f(b)^2 is even; if f(b) is odd, f(b)^2 is odd. So we need to check parity.

But we also have the condition that f(b) must be such that b^a - f(b)^2 is even. That is, b^a and f(b)^2 have same parity. Since b^a parity = parity of b (if a≥1). So we need:

- If b is odd, then b^a is odd → f(b)^2 must be odd → f(b) must be odd.
- If b is even, then b^a is even → f(b)^2 must be even → f(b) must be even.

Now, does our assignment satisfy that? For odd b, f(b)=1 (odd). For even b, f(b) is either 4 (b=2) or 2 (b≥4 even). Both are even. So indeed parity condition holds. Therefore, for any even a ≥ 4, the condition 2 | (b^a - f(b)^2) holds for all b. So that's good.

Now, check a = 2, f(2)=4. Need 4 | (b^2 - f(b)^4) for all b.

We need to verify that for all b ∈ N, 4 divides b^2 - f(b)^4.

We have f(b) as defined. Let's compute b^2 mod 4 and f(b)^4 mod 4.

b^2 mod 4:

- If b is even, b^2 ≡ 0 mod 4.
- If b is odd, b^2 ≡ 1 mod 4.

f(b)^4 mod 4:

- If f(b) is even, then f(b)^4 is divisible by 16, thus ≡ 0 mod 4.
- If f(b) is odd, then f(b)^4 ≡ 1 mod 4.

So the condition 4 | (b^2 - f(b)^4) is equivalent to:

- If b is odd: b^2 ≡ 1, need f(b)^4 ≡ 1 → f(b) odd.
- If b is even: b^2 ≡ 0, need f(b)^4 ≡ 0 → f(b) even.

Our assignment:

- b odd → f(b)=1 (odd) → f(b)^4 ≡ 1. So condition holds (difference ≡ 0).
- b even → f(b) is either 4 (b=2) or 2 (b≥4 even). Both are even, so f(b)^4 ≡ 0. So condition holds.

Thus a=2 condition satisfied.

Therefore, this function is bonza. So we have exhibited a bonza function where f(2)=4 > 2, and for n=2, f(2)/2 = 2. For n=4, f(4)=2 ≤ 4, ratio 0.5. For n=6, f(6)=2 ≤ 6, ratio 0.333... Now, to find the supremum of f(n)/n over all bonza functions, we need to consider the worst-case ratio. The function we constructed with f(2)=4, f(4)=16, f(6)=8, etc. has ratios: 2, 4, 1.333..., but we need to see if any bonza function can have f(n)/n > 4 for some n. For n=2, max is 2. For n=4, max is 4. For n=8, max is 4 (if f(8)=32 gives 4). Could we have n=16? 3^16 - 1 is huge, but does it contain a factor of 2^? Actually we can examine the exponent of 2 in 3^n - 1. For n even, 3^n - 1 = (3^{n/2} - 1)(3^{n/2} + 1). Since 3^{n/2} is odd, the difference is even. The exponent of 2 in 3^n - 1 is something like v2(3^n - 1) = v2(3^{n/2} - 1) + v2(3^{n/2} + 1) - 1? Actually for odd a, v2(a^k - 1) is something like v2(a - 1) + v2(k) - 1) for a ≡ 1 mod 2, but more precisely if a is odd, then v2(a^m - 1) = v2(a - 1) + v2(m) - 1? Let's recall the LTE lemma: For odd a, b, and a ≡ b mod 2, but more standard: For odd a, b, and a ≡ b (mod 2), then v2(a^n - b^n) = v2(a - b) + v2(n) - 1 for n even? Actually the LTE for p=2 requires a ≡ b (mod 2) and a ≠ b. For odd a,b, a ≡ b ≡ 1 mod 2. But we need a ≡ b (mod 2) which holds. The LTE for p=2 has a formula: If a and b are odd, then v2(a^n - b^n) = v2(a - b) + v2(n) for n even? Let's recall the precise LTE lemma for p=2: For odd a,b, and n even, v2(a^n - b^n) = v2(a - b) + v2(a + b) + v2(n) - 1? Actually we need to be careful. The LTE lemma for p=2 is special. The standard LTE for p=2: Let a and b be odd integers, and n be even. Then v2(a^n - b^n) = v2(a - b) + v2(a + b) + v2(n) - 1. But that's for a^n - b^n. However we have a^n - 1 = a^n - 1^n. So b=1, which is odd. Then a^n - 1^n. For n even, we can apply LTE with a and b=1. But then a - b = a - 1, a + b = a + 1. So v2(a^n - 1) = v2(a - 1) + v2(a + 1) + v2(n) - 1. For a=3, a-1=2, a+1=4. v2(2)=1, v2(4)=2. Then v2(3^n - 1) = 1 + 2 + v2(n) - 1 = 2 + v2(n). For n even, v2(n) ≥ 1. So v2(3^n - 1) = 2 + v2(n). For n=2, v2=1 → 3^2-1=8 → v2=3, 2+1=3 ok. n=4, v2=2 → 2+2=4 → 3^4-1=80, v2=4 ok. n=6, v2=1 → 2+1=3 → 3^6-1=728, v2=3 ok. n=8, v2=3 → 2+3=5 → 3^8-1=6560, v2=5 ok. So indeed the maximum power of 2 dividing 3^n - 1 is 2^{2+v2(n)}. So for n = 2^k (k≥1), v2(n)=k, then v2(3^n-1) = 2 + k. Then the largest power of 2 dividing 3^n-1 is 2^{2+k}. Then f(n) could be that power of 2, as long as it divides n^n. Since n is a power of 2, n^n is a huge power of 2. So f(n) can be up to 2^{2+v2(n)}. Then f(n)/n = 2^{2+v2(n)} / 2^{v2(n)} = 2^{2+v2(n)} / 2^{v2(n)} = 2^{2} = 4. So indeed for n = 2^k, the maximum possible f(n) (as a divisor of 3^n-1) is 2^{2+k}. Then f(n)/n = 4. So ratio 4 is achievable.

Now, what about n that are not powers of 2? For n even, v2(n) is some number t. Then the largest power of 2 dividing 3^n-1 is 2^{2+t}. So f(n) could be up to that, but also must divide n^n. Since n^n has factor 2^{n * v2(n)} (since n = 2^{t} * u with u odd, then n^n has factor 2^{n * t}). That's huge. So the restriction is from 3^n-1, not from n^n. So the maximum f(n) for even n is at most 2^{2+v2(n)}. Then f(n)/n = 2^{2+v2(n)} / (2^{t} * u) = 2^{2+t} / (2^{t} * u) = 2^{2} / u = 4 / u. Since u is odd, u≥1. So f(n)/n ≤ 4 / u. For n = 2^k * u with u odd, u≥1. So the maximum ratio occurs when u=1 (i.e., n is a power of 2). Then ratio ≤ 4. So indeed, if f(3)=1, then for even n, f(n) ≤ 2^{2+v2(n)}. That's a bound. So c ≥ 4.

But is it possible that f(3) is not 1? We have f(3) must be 1 or 3, as argued. If f(3)=3, then a=3,b=5 condition gives f(3) divides 5^3 - f(5)^{3} = 125 - f(5)^3. "Let $\mathbb{N}$ denote the set of positive integers. A function $f\colon\mathbb{N}\to\mathbb{N}$ is said to be bonza if
\[
f(a)\quad\text{divides}\quad b^a-f(b)^{f(a)}
\]
for all positive integers $a$ and $b$.

Determine the smallest real constant $c$ such that $f(n)\leqslant cn$ for all bonza functions $f$ and all positive integers $n$."

We need to find c. So we need to compute sup_{f bonza, n} f(n)/n. The example we gave has f(2^k) = 2^{k+2} for k≥2, but note that for k=2 (n=4), f(4)=16, ratio 4. For k=3 (n=8), f(8)=32, ratio 4. So v2(3^n - 3^{f(n)}) = v2(3^{min(n,f(n))}) + v2(3^{|n-f(n)|} - 1) = min(n,f(n)) * v2(3) (which is 0) + v2(3^{|n-f(n)|} - 1). Since 3 is odd, v2(3)=0. So v2 of the difference is v2(3^{|n-f(n)|} - 1). That's determined by LTE: v2(3^{k} - 1) = v2(3 - 1) + v2(3 + 1) + v2(k) - 1? Actually for odd a, v2(a^k - 1) = v2(a - 1) + v2(a + 1) + v2(k) - 1 for k even, and v2(a - 1) + v2(k) for k odd, with a=3. For k even, v2(3^k - 1) = v2(3 - 1) + v2(3 + 1) + v2(k) - 1 = 1 + 2 + v2(k) - 1 = 2 + v2(k). So indeed v2(3^k - 1) ≥ 1 + v2(k) (with equality when k is odd), and for even k, it's 2 + v2(k). So indeed gcd(n^n, 3^n-1) ≤ 4n, with equality for n a power of 2. That's a known bound. So if we can prove that f(3) must be 1, then we have f(n) ≤ gcd(n^n, 3^n-1) ≤ 4n. So c ≤ 4. That would be a clean proof.

So f(8)=32 is allowed. So that gives ratio 4. So indeed, f(3)=3 doesn't allow higher ratio than 4. So the supremum is 4.

Thus the proof can be structured as:

1. Show f(1)=1.

Thus it appears that the function defined as:

f(n) = 1 for odd n,
f(2) = 4,
f(n) = 2 for even n ≥ 4,

is a bonza function. Let's double-check some edge cases: n=1 (odd) → f(1)=1. n=2 (even) → f(2)=4. n=3 (odd) → f(3)=1. n=4 (even) → f(4)=2. n=5 (odd) → f(5)=1. n=6 (even) → f(6)=2. n=7 (odd) → f(7)=1. n=8 (even) → f(8)=2. etc.

Now, is there any a,b such that f(a) does not divide b^a - f(b)^{f(a)}? We need to check systematically.

We have:

- If a is odd, f(a)=1. Then condition becomes 1 divides b^a - f(b)^1 = b^a - f(b). That's always true. So all pairs with a odd are automatically satisfied.

- If a is even, f(a) is either 4 (a=2) or 2 (a≥4 even). So we need to check for a even:

Case 1: a = 2, f(2) = 4. Then condition: 4 | (b^2 - f(b)^4) for all b ∈ N.

Case 2: a ≥ 4 even, f(a) = 2. Then condition: 2 | (b^a - f(b)^2) for all b ∈ N.

Now, we need to check that these divisibilities hold for all b. Let's analyze.

For a ≥ 4 even, f(a)=2. Then we need 2 divides b^a - f(b)^2. Since b^a is odd if b is odd, even if b is even. f(b) is either 1 (if b odd) or 2 or 4 (if b even). But f(b)^2 mod 2: any integer squared mod 2 is its parity, so if f(b) is even, f(b)^2 is even; if f(b) is odd, f(b)^2 is odd. So we need to check parity.

But we also have the condition that f(b) must be such that b^a - f(b)^2 is even. That is, b^a and f(b)^2 have same parity. Since b^a parity = parity of b (if a≥1). So we need:

- If b is odd, then b^a is odd → f(b)^2 must be odd → f(b) must be odd.
- If b is even, then b^a is even → f(b)^2 must be even → f(b) must be even.

Now, does our assignment satisfy that? For odd b, f(b)=1 (odd). For even b, f(b) is either 4 (b=2) or 2 (b≥4 even). Both are even. So indeed parity condition holds. Therefore, for any even a ≥ 4, the condition 2 | (b^a - f(b)^2) holds for all b. So that's good.

Now, check a = 2, f(2)=4. Need 4 | (b^2 - f(b)^4) for all b.

We need to verify that for all b ∈ N, 4 divides b^2 - f(b)^4.

We have f(b) as defined. Let's compute b^2 mod 4 and f(b)^4 mod 4.

b^2 mod 4:

- If b is even, b^2 ≡ 0 mod 4.
- If b is odd, b^2 ≡ 1 mod 4.

f(b)^4 mod 4:

- If f(b) is even, then f(b)^4 is divisible by 16, thus ≡ 0 mod 4.
- If f(b) is odd, then f(b)^4 ≡ 1 mod 4.

So the condition 4 | (b^2 - f(b)^4) is equivalent to:

- If b is odd: b^2 ≡ 1, need f(b)^4 ≡ 1 → f(b) odd.
- If b is even: b^2 ≡ 0, need f(b)^4 ≡ 0 → f(b) even.

Our assignment:

- b odd → f(b)=1 (odd) → f(b)^4 ≡ 1. So condition holds (difference ≡ 0).
- b even → f(b) is either 4 (b=2) or 2 (b≥4 even). Both are even, so f(b)^4 ≡ 0. So condition holds.

Thus a=2 condition satisfied.

Therefore, this function is bonza. So we have exhibited a bonza function where f(2)=4 > 2, and for n=2, f(2)/2 = 2. For n=4, f(4)=2 ≤ 4, ratio 0.5. For n=6, f(6)=2 ≤ 6, ratio 0.333... So the maximum ratio we have so far is 2, achieved at n=2. Could there be a bonza function with even larger ratio, say f(2)=something like 8? But we already deduced from a=2,b=2 that f(2) must divide 4. So f(2) ∈ {1,2,4}. So ratio 2 is max for n=2. For n=3, we forced f(3)=1 if f(2)=4, but maybe if f(2) is smaller, f(3) could be 3 (ratio 1). So ratio 1 is max for n=3. For n=4, f(4) could be 4 (if identity), or maybe 8? But from a=4,b=4, f(4) divides 256, so could be 8, 16, etc. However we also have condition a=4,b=2: f(4) divides 16 - 4^{f(4)}. As derived, if d divides 4^d, then d divides 16. If f(3) is something like 1 or 3, etc. We'll need to see if there exists a bonza function with f(4) > 4 (like 8). That would give f(4)/4 = 2, which is larger than 1. But we need to ensure overall existence. Could we have a bonza function where f(2)=4, f(4)=8, and f(3)=1? Let's test a=2,b=4: 4 divides 16 - f(4)^4 = 16 - 8^4 = 16 - 4096 = -4080, divisible by 4? 4080/4=1020, yes. a=4,b=2: 8 divides 16 - 4^8 = 16 - 65536 = -65520. 65520/8 = 8190, yes. a=4,b=3: 8 divides 81 - f(3)^4. But we also have a=6,b=2: f(6) divides 64 - 4^{f(6)}. So we can attempt to set f(6)=2 maybe. But we must also check a=4,b=6: f(4)=8 divides 6^4 - f(6)^8 = 1296 - 2^8 = 1296 - 256 = 1040. 1040 / 8 = 130, so divisible. a=6,b=4: f(6)=2 divides 4^6 - f(4)^2 = 4096 - 64 = 4032, divisible by 2. So far okay. However we also need to check a=4,b=5: 8 divides 625 - f(5)^4. f(5) could be 1, then 625-1=624, 8 divides 624? 624/8=78, yes. So okay. But is there any restriction from a=2,b=4 that forces f(4) to be something like 2,4? We already saw that for any f(4) even, a=2,b=4 condition (4 divides 16 - f(4)^4) holds if f(4) is even, because 16 - (even)^4 is divisible by 16? Actually (even)^4 is divisible by 16, so 16 - (multiple of 16) is 0 mod 16, thus divisible by 4. So any even f(4) works. So f(4) can be any even divisor of 256, not necessarily ≤4. So indeed we could have f(4)=8,16,... up to 256. But we also have a=4,b=2 condition: f(4) must divide 16 - 4^{f(4)}. That's a restriction. Let's analyze that.

We have f(4) = d. Condition: d | (16 - 4^d). Since 4^d is a power of 2. Write d = 2^t * u where u is odd. Then 4^d = 2^{2d}. The divisibility d | (16 - 2^{2d}) is a congruence. We can try to find necessary conditions.

We can consider modulo d. Since 4^d is huge. But we can use the fact that if d is a power of 2, say d = 2^k. Then 4^d = 2^{2 * 2^k} = 2^{2^{k+1}}. Then 16 - 2^{2^{k+1}}. Does d divide that? Let's try to prove that for any bonza f, we have f(n) ≤ 4n. Suppose there exists n with f(n) > 4n. Consider the smallest such n. Then for all k < n, f(k) ≤ 4k. Now, we need to derive a contradiction using some pair (a,b). Possibly we can consider a = n and b = 2. Then f(n) divides 2^n - f(2)^{f(n)}. Since f(2) is at most 4, we can analyze the 2-adic valuation of that difference. If f(n) > 4n, then in particular, f(n) > 2n. That suggests that f(n) is larger than something. But maybe we can get a bound on f(n) in terms of n by considering the size of the difference: |2^n - f(2)^{f(n)}| ≥ something like (f(2)^{f(n)} - 2^n). But divisibility doesn't give size.

Alternatively, consider a = n and b = 3. Then f(n) divides 3^n - f(3)^{f(n)}. Using LTE, we can bound v2(f(n)) from above. But we need to bound f(n) itself. Perhaps we can combine with the condition that f(n) divides n^n. Then if f(n) > 4n, then n^n is huge, but that's okay. However maybe we can show that f(n) must be a divisor of something like 2^n - 4^{f(n)} and also that f(n) divides n^n. Then if f(n) is large, its 2-adic valuation is large, but also it must have odd factors. But the odd part of 2^n - 4^{f(n)} is odd and can be large. So not a contradiction.

I think we need a more clever argument. Let's reexamine the problem statement:

"Let $\mathbb{N}$ denote the set of positive integers. A function $f\colon\mathbb{N}\to\mathbb{N}$ is said to be bonza if
\[
f(a)\quad\text{divides}\quad b^a-f(b)^{f(a)}
\]
for all positive integers $a$ and $b$.

Determine the smallest real constant $c$ such that $f(n)\leqslant cn$ for all bonza functions $f$ and all positive integers $n$."

We need to find c. So we need to compute sup_{f bonza, n} f(n)/n. The example we gave has f(2^k) = 2^{k+2} for k≥2, but note that for k=2 (n=4), f(4)=16, ratio 4. For k=3 (n=8), f(8)=32, ratio 4. For k=4 (n=16), f(16)=64, ratio 4. So indeed ratio 4 is achieved. So c ≥ 4. Now, can we achieve ratio > 4? For n = 2^k * u with u odd > 1, can we have f(n) > 4n? For n=6, 4n=24. Could f(6) be 27? That would be 4.5n. But f(6) must divide 6^6=46656, so 27 is 3^3, but 27 divides 46656? 46656 / 27 = 1728, yes. So that's allowed. But would such a function be bonza? We need to check all pairs. The condition with b=3 and a=6: f(6) divides 3^6 - f(3)^{f(6)}. If f(3) is 1, then 3^6 - 1 = 728, and 27 does not divide 728. If f(3) is 3, then 3^6 - 3^{27} is huge, but does 27 divide it? 3^6 = 729. 3^{27} is huge. 3^{27} mod 27? Since 3^3=27, 3^{27} is divisible by 27. So 729 - multiple of 27 is 729 mod 27 = 0, so difference divisible by 27. That suggests that with f(3)=3, f(6)=27 might be possible. But also need to satisfy a=6,b=2: f(6) divides 64 - f(2)^{27}. With f(2) could be 4. Then 64 - 4^{27} is huge, but modulo 27 we can compute. 4^{27} mod 27? Since 4^3=64≡10, 4^6≡19, 4^9≡1, so order 9. Then 4^{27} ≡ 4^{27 mod 9 = 0} ≡ 1. So 64 - 1 = 63, which is divisible by 27? 63/27 not integer. So fails. If f(2)=2, then 2^{27} mod 27? 2^? φ(27)=18, 2^18≡1, 2^27 = 2^18 * 2^9 ≡ 2^9 = 512 ≡ 512-27*18=512-486=26, then 64-26=38, not divisible by 27. If f(2)=1, then 64-1=63, not divisible by 27. So f(6)=27 fails. So not all large odd divisors are possible.

Thus the bound may indeed be 4. But we need a proof.

Let's try to derive a bound from the condition with a = n and b = 3. Using LTE, we can get:

If f(3) = 1, then f(n) divides 3^n - 1. Then any prime p dividing f(n) must satisfy p | 3^n - 1. In particular, if p is an odd prime, then the order of 3 modulo p divides n. Also, p may be 2 (if f(n) is even). For p=2, v2(f(n)) ≤ v2(3^n - 1). That's at most 2 + v2(n) (as computed). So v2(f(n)) ≤ 2 + v2(n). That's not a bound on f(n) itself, but on its 2-adic part. f(n) | 2^n - f(2)^{f(n)}.   (A)

Similarly, a = n and b = 3:

f(n) | 3^n - f(3)^{f(n)}.   (B)

## Solution
Let $\mathbb{N}=\{1,2,3,\dots\}$.  A function $f:\mathbb{N}\to\mathbb{N}$ is called **bonza** if

\[
f(a)\mid b^{\,a}-f(b)^{\,f(a)}\qquad\text{for all }a,b\in\mathbb{N}.
\]

We are to determine the smallest real constant $c$ such that $f(n)\le c\,n$ for every bonza $f$ and every $n\in\mathbb{N}$.

---

### 1.  Basic consequences
**$f(1)=1$.**  
Take $a=b=1$: $f(1)\mid 1^{\,1}-f(1)^{\,f(1)} = 1-f(1)^{f(1)}$.  
Because $f(1)^{f(1)}$ is a multiple of $f(1)$, we obtain $f(1)\mid 1$.  Hence $f(1)=1$.

**For any $a$ we have $f(a)\mid a^{\,a}$.**  
Put $b=a$; then $f(a)\mid a^{\,a}-f(a)^{f(a)}$.  Since $f(a)\mid f(a)^{f(a)}$, it follows that $f(a)\mid a^{\,a}$.  Thus every prime divisor of $f(a)$ also divides $a$.

---

### 2.  Small values of $f(2)$ and $f(3)$  
From $f(2)\mid 2^{2}=4$ we get $f(2)\in\{1,2,4\}$.

From $f(3)\mid 3^{3}=27$ we get $f(3)\in\{1,3,9,27\}$.  We will later show that $9$ and $27$ are impossible; the only possible values are $1$ and $3$.

---

### 3.  A necessary bound using $b=3$

Take $n$ arbitrary.  From $a=n,\;b=3$ we have

\[
f(n)\mid 3^{\,n}-f(3)^{\,f(n)}. \tag{1}
\]

We distinguish two cases according to $f(3)$.

#### Case $f(3)=1$
Then (1) becomes $f(n)\mid 3^{\,n}-1$.  By the LTE lemma for the prime $2$,

\[
v_{2}(3^{\,n}-1)=v_{2}(3-1)+v_{2}(3+1)+v_{2}(n)-1 = 1+2+v_{2}(n)-1 = 2+v_{2}(n) .
\]

Thus $f(n)$ can contain at most $2+v_{2}(n)$ factors $2$; consequently

\[
f(n)\le 2^{\,2+v_{2}(n)} \cdot \prod_{p\mid n,\;p>2} p^{\,v_{p}(f(n)) .
\]

But also $f(n)\mid n^{\,n}$, so every odd prime $p$ dividing $f(n)$ divides $n$.  Therefore $f(n)$ is a divisor of $\gcd(n^{\,n},\,3^{\,n}-1)$.  Let us denote

\[
G(n)=\gcd\bigl(n^{\,n},\,3^{\,n}-1\bigr).
\]

We will compute $G(n)$ later; for now note that $G(n)$ is a divisor of $2^{\,2+v_{2}(n)}$ because $3^{\,n}-1$ is not divisible by any odd prime that does not divide $n$? Actually we need a precise bound.  Let's proceed.

#### Case $f(3)=3$
Then (1) becomes $f(n)\mid 3^{\,n}-3^{\,f(n)}$.  Write $f(n)=2^{a}\cdot u$ with $u$ odd.  The difference $3^{\,n}-3^{\,f(n)}=3^{\min(n,f(n))}\bigl(1-3^{\,|n-f(n)|}\bigr)$.  The $3$-adic valuation of the difference is $\min(n,f(n))$ (since $1-3^{\,k}$ is not divisible by $3$).  Hence $v_{3}(f(n))\le\min(n,f(n))$.  This bound is far from linear.  However we also have the relation coming from $a=3,\;b=n$:

\[
f(3)\mid n^{\,3}-f(n)^{\,f(3)} \quad\Longrightarrow\quad 3\mid n^{\,3}-f(n)^{3}. \tag{2}
\]

Now, $n^{3}\equiv n\pmod 3$ and $f(n)^{3}\equiv f(n)\pmod 3$ (since $x^{3}\equiv x\pmod 3$).  So (2) gives $n\equiv f(n)\pmod 3$.

---

### 4.  Proving that $f(3)$ must be $1$

We show that $f(3)=3$ leads to a contradiction.  Suppose $f(3)=3$.

**Step 1.**  From $a=3,\;b=2$ we obtain $3\mid 2^{3}-f(2)^{3} = 8-f(2)^{3}$.  
Since $f(2)\in\{1,2,4\}$, compute $f(2)^{3}\bmod 3$:
\[
1^{3}\equiv1,\quad 2^{3}=8\equiv2,\quad 4^{3}=64\equiv1.
\]
Thus $8-f(2)^{3}\equiv 2-1=1$ or $2-2=0$ or $2-1=1$ modulo $3$.  The congruence $3\mid 8-f(2)^{3}$ forces $f(2)^{3}\equiv2\pmod3$, i.e. $f(2)\equiv2\pmod3$.  The only possibility is $f(2)=2$ (since $2\equiv2$, $1\equiv1$, $4\equiv1$).  Hence $f(2)=2$.

**Step 2.**  Consider $a=3,\;b=5$: $3\mid 5^{3}-f(5)^{3}=125-f(5)^{3}$.  Therefore $f(5)^{3}\equiv125\equiv2\pmod3$, so $f(5)\equiv2\pmod3$.  But $f(5)\mid5^{5}=3125$, hence $f(5)$ is a power of $5$.  Powers of $5$ modulo $3$ are $5^{1}\equiv2$, $5^{2}\equiv1$, $5^{3}\equiv2$, etc.  So $f(5)\equiv2\pmod3$ is possible only when the exponent is odd, i.e. $f(5)=5,125,3125,\dots$.

**Step 3.**  Use $a=5,\;b=3$: $f(5)\mid 3^{5}-f(3)^{f(5)} = 243-3^{\,f(5)}$.  For $f(5)=5$ we get $243-3^{5}=243-243=0$, which is divisible by $5$; the condition holds.  For $f(5)=25$ (which is $5^{2}$, but we are considering odd exponents; $25$ is even exponent, so $f(5)=25$ would be $\equiv1\pmod3$, contradiction to Step 2.  So $f(5)$ must be $5,125,3125,\dots$ – odd powers of $5$.  Now test $f(5)=125$: $243-3^{125}$ has $3$-adic valuation $5$ (since $3^{5}=243$ and $3^{125}$ has valuation $125$), so $3^{5}\mid243-3^{125}$; but we need $125$ to divide that difference.  Compute the $5$-adic valuation of $3^{125}-3^{5}=3^{5}(3^{120}-1)$.  $3^{4}\equiv1\pmod5$ and $3^{4}-1=80$ is divisible by $5$; $v_{5}(3^{4}-1)=1$, and $v_{5}(120)=1$, so $v_{5}(3^{120}-1)=2$.  Thus $v_{5}(3^{125}-3^{5})=2$, so $125$ does **not** divide $3^{125}-3^{5}$ (since $v_{5}=2<3$).  Hence $f(5)=125$ fails.  Similarly, higher odd powers of $5$ will also have $v_{5}(3^{f(5)}-3^{5})=2$ (by LTE, because $f(5)-5$ is a multiple of $4$ and $v_{5}(3^{4}-1)=1$, $v_{5}((f(5)-5)/4)=1$), so the difference is not divisible by $5^{3}=125$.  Thus the only candidate that might satisfy $f(5)\mid243-3^{f(5)}$ is $f(5)=5$ (or $1$, but $1$ fails $a=5,b=2$ as shown later).  So $f(5)=5$ is forced.

**Step 4.**  Now consider $a=5,\;b=2$: $f(5)=5$ must divide $2^{5}-f(2)^{5}=32-2^{5}=32-32=0$, which is true.  So far consistent.

**Step 5.**  The critical contradiction comes from $a=3,\;b=5$: we already used that to deduce $3\mid125-f(5)^{3}$, which is satisfied.  But we also need to check $a=3,\;b=8$? That's not contradictory.  However we have not yet used $a=2,\;b=3$? That gives $2\mid9-3^{2}=0$.  So fine.  The issue is that with $f(3)=3$ and $f(2)=2$, $f(5)=5$, $f(4)=16$, $f(8)=32$, etc., we can attempt to construct a function.  But the existence of such a function would contradict a known fact?  Actually we need to prove that $f(3)=3$ is impossible.  Let's examine the condition with $a=2$ and $b=3$ we already did.  The condition with $a=3$ and $b=2$ is fine.  So why would $f(3)=3$ be impossible?  The earlier analysis suggested that with $f(3)=3$, we could have $f(5)=5$, $f(4)=16$, $f(8)=32$, etc., and all those seem consistent.  But we also have to satisfy $a=3,b= something$ like $n$ for all $n$.  The function we described might indeed be bonza.  So maybe $f(3)=3$ is possible.  But then, we need to prove that $f(n)\le4n$ still holds.  Let's test with $n=4$, $f(4)=16$ works.  $n=8$, $f(8)=32$ works.  $n=5$, $f(5)=5$ works.  $n=7$, $f(7)=1$ works.  $n=9$, $f(9)=9$?  But need to check $a=9,b=3$: $f(9)$ must divide $3^9-3^{f(9)} = 19683-3^{f(9)}$.  If $f(9)=9$, then $3^9-3^9=0$, divisible.  But also $a=9,b=2$: $f(9)$ divides $2^9-2^{f(9)} = 512-2^9=0$, works.  So $f(9)=9$ possible.  So it seems we can have $f(n)=n$ for all $n$, which is fine.  But we also have $f(4)=16$, which is not identity.  So the function is not identity.  We need to test whether the function with $f(3)=3$ and $f(2)=2$ can be extended to all $n$ while maintaining the condition.  Let's attempt to define $f$ as:

\[
f(n)=\begin{cases}
n & \text{if } n \text{ is not a multiple of } 4,\\
2n & \text{if } n \text{ is a multiple of } 4 \text{ but not of } 8? 
\end{cases}
\]

But we saw that $f(4)=16$ (which is $4n$).  For $n=8$, $f(8)=32$ ($4n$).  For $n=12$, $f(12)=?$  If we set $f(12)=12$, does it satisfy $a=12,b=3$ with $f(3)=3$?  $12$ is even, not a power of $2$, but $f(12)=12$ would be okay.  However we must also check $a=12,b=2$: $12$ divides $2^{12}-2^{12}=0$, okay.  So $f(12)=12$ works.  So the function could be:

- $f(n)=n$ for all $n$ except that $f(2^k)=2^{k+2}$ for $k\ge2$ (i.e., $f(4)=16$, $f(8)=32$, $f(16)=64$, ...).  But then we also need to define $f(2)=?$  We have $f(2)=2$ (since $f(2)=2$ forced by $f(3)=3$).  So that's consistent.  Then $f(4)=16$, $f(8)=32$, etc.  This function is not the identity, but it seems plausible.  However we need to verify all pairs, especially those where $a$ is a power of $2$ and $b$ is something like $n$ that is not a power of $2$, and $a$ is not a power of $2$, etc.  I suspect it's bonza.  But we must confirm the condition $a=8,b=3$: $f(8)=32$ divides $3^8-3^{32}$, which we already argued works.  $a=8,b=5$ works.  $a=8,b=6$ works.  $a=8,b=7$ works.  $a=8,b=8$ trivial.  So seems okay.  So $f(3)=3$ may be possible.  Then we need to prove the bound $f(n)\le4n$ for all $n$ regardless of $f(3)$.

  But note: In the function with $f(3)=3$, $f(4)=16$ gives $16=4\cdot4$, so bound holds.  $f(8)=32$ gives $32=4\cdot8$, holds.  $f(2)=2$ gives $2<8$.  $f(5)=5$ gives $5<20$.  So the bound holds.  So the worst-case ratio is $4$, achieved when $n$ is a power of $2$ and $f(n)=2^{k+2}$ (with $f(3)=1$).  So the constant $c$ must be at least $4$, and we need to prove that $c=4$ works.

  Thus, we need to prove that for any bonza $f$, we have $f(n)\le 4n$ for all $n$.  The previous case analysis with $f(3)=1$ gives $f(n)\le 2^{2+v_2(n)}$ because $f(n)$ divides $3^n-1$ and also divides $n^n$, but we need to bound the odd part as well.  Let's do it more carefully.

  Since $f(n)\mid n^n$, any prime $p$ dividing $f(n)$ divides $n$.  Also, $f(n)\mid 3^n-1$ (if $f(3)=1$).  Therefore $f(n)\mid \gcd(n^n,\,3^n-1)$.  Let $G(n)=\gcd(n^n,\,3^n-1)$.  We will compute $G(n)$ and show $G(n)\le 4n$.  Then $f(n)\le G(n)\le 4n$, so the bound holds.

  For the case $f(3)=3$, we need a different bound.  Perhaps we can show that $f(3)=3$ leads to a contradiction using a more sophisticated argument.  Let's attempt to find a contradiction with $f(3)=3$ by considering $n=5$ and $n=7$ etc.  We already saw that with $f(3)=3$, $f(2)=2$ is forced.  Then we examined $f(5)$ and deduced $f(5)=5$ is possible.  But also $f(5)$ must satisfy $a=5,b=3$: $f(5)\mid 3^5-3^{f(5)}=243-3^{f(5)}$.  For $f(5)=5$, we have $243-3^5=243-243=0$, which is divisible by $5$, okay.  So $f(5)=5$ works.  Now, consider $a=5,b=2$: $5$ divides $32-2^5=0$, works.  So far consistent.  Now, consider $a=3,b=5$ we already did.  So no contradiction yet.  But we also have $a=3,b=8$ etc.  So maybe $f(3)=3$ is indeed possible.  Let's test the condition $a=3,b=4$: $3$ divides $4^3 - f(4)^3$.  If $f(4)=16$, then $64 - 4096 = -4032$, divisible by $3$?  $4032$ sum digits $9$, yes.  So works.  $a=3,b=6$: $3$ divides $216 - 6^3 = 0$, works.  $a=3,b=7$: $3$ divides $343 - 7^3 = 0$, works.  $a=3,b=8$: $3$ divides $512 - 32^3 = 512 - 32768 = -32256$, divisible by $3$, works.  So all fine.

  So it seems $f(3)=3$ can exist.  Then the bound $f(n)\le4n$ must be proved for this case as well.  Let's attempt to derive a bound using the condition with $b=2$ and $b=3$ combined with the fact that $f(n)\mid n^n$.  For any $n$, we have:

  - $f(n)\mid 2^n - f(2)^{f(n)}$.
  - $f(n)\mid 3^n - f(3)^{f(n)}$.

  If $f(3)=3$, then the second gives $f(n)\mid 3^n - 3^{f(n)}$.  Using LTE, we can bound the $3$-adic valuation, but not the size.  However we also have the relation from $a=2,b=n$: $f(2)\mid n^2 - f(n)^{f(2)}$.  With $f(2)=2$ (forced), this gives $2\mid n^2 - f(n)^2$, i.e., $n^2\equiv f(n)^2\pmod2$.  This is automatically satisfied because $n^2$ and $f(n)^2$ have same parity.  That's trivial.

  So we need a better approach.  Perhaps we can prove that $f(3)=1$ is forced.  Let's examine the possibility of $f(3)=3$ more deeply.  Suppose $f(3)=3$.  Then $f(2)=2$ as shown.  Consider $a=3,b=2$ gave $3\mid 8-2^3=0$, okay.  Now, consider $a=3,b=5$ gave $3\mid 125-f(5)^3$.  So $f(5)\equiv2\pmod3$.  But $f(5)$ must be a power of $5$ because $f(5)\mid5^5$.  Powers of $5$ modulo $3$ are $2,1,2,1,\dots$.  So $f(5)$ must be an odd power of $5$ (i.e., exponent odd).  So $f(5)=5,125,3125,\dots$.  Now, also consider $a=5,b=3$: $f(5)\mid 3^5-3^{f(5)}=243-3^{f(5)}$.  For $f(5)=5$, this is $243-3^5=0$, okay.  For $f(5)=125$, $243-3^{125}$ is huge.  But we need $125$ to divide that.  As we computed, $v_5(3^{125}-3^5)=2$, so $125$ does not divide it.  Hence $f(5)=125$ fails.  Similarly, any higher odd power of $5$ will have $v_5=2$ because $3^{f(5)-5} - 1$ has $v_5=2$ (by LTE).  Therefore $f(5)$ must be $5$.  So $f(5)=5$ is forced.

  Now, consider $a=3,b=8$: $3\mid 8^3 - f(8)^3 = 512 - f(8)^3$.  So $f(8)^3\equiv512\equiv2\pmod3$ (since $512\bmod3=2$).  Thus $f(8)\equiv2
